[4] Thomas Nagel, *The View from Nowhere* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1986). Nagel's term names the aspiration to achieve a perspective that transcends any particular point of view—to see the world as it is independently of how it appears from any situated location.

[5] René Descartes, *Meditations on First Philosophy* (1641), especially the Second Meditation, where Descartes discovers the *cogito* as the one indubitable certainty, and the Sixth Meditation, where he argues for the real distinction between mind and body.


## Chapter 6: The View from Within

[6] Edmund Husserl, *The Crisis of European Sciences and Transcendental Phenomenology* (1936). Husserl diagnoses modern science's "crisis" as stemming from its forgetting of the life-world (*Lebenswelt*)—the pre-theoretical world of lived experience that science presupposes but cannot thematize from within its objectivist framework.


## Chapter 7: Situatedness

[7] Martin Heidegger, *Being and Time* (1927), Division I, Chapter 3 (§§14-24). Heidegger's analysis of worldhood and being-in-the-world establishes that Dasein (human existence) is always already situated in a world of meaningful involvements, not a detached subject confronting bare objects.

[8] Hans Jonas, *The Phenomenon of Life: Toward a Philosophical Biology* (Evanston: Northwestern University Press, 1966), especially "The Meaning of Metabolism" and "Life, Death, and the Body in the Theory of Being." Jonas argues that metabolism constitutes the most basic form of selfhood and stakes — the organism, never identical to its momentary material substrate, maintains its form by continuously exchanging its matter, so that its being is a task held off against dissolution rather than a given fact; this metabolic exposure to non-being is the origin of concern. The book takes Jonas for this *metabolic* grounding of concern and is careful (§24b) to distinguish it from the *reflective* relation to one's own mortality — Heidegger's being-toward-death — which is a distinct register; Jonas, Heidegger's student, himself criticized Heidegger for intellectualizing existence while neglecting the organic body. Jonas's grounding tells against the machine on his own terms: in the same volume his critique of cybernetics argues that feedback and control without metabolic selfhood yield the simulation of purposiveness, not its reality — which makes Jonas, like IIT in §55-bis.4, an independent witness for the book's exclusion rather than merely a source it draws on.

[9] Maurice Merleau-Ponty, *Phenomenology of Perception* (1945), Part One, "The Body." Merleau-Ponty's account of the "body schema" and the "habitual body" establishes that our bodily existence is not merely biological but constitutes our primary mode of being-in-the-world.


## Chapter 8: The Krisis

[10] The term *krisis* (Greek: κρίσις) means decision, judgment, turning point. Husserl uses it in the title of his final work to name the critical juncture at which European sciences and humanity find themselves. We use it here to name decision as the turning point that discloses truth.

[11] Søren Kierkegaard, *Either/Or* (1843) and *Fear and Trembling* (1843). Kierkegaard's analysis of the "leap" and the distinction between aesthetic, ethical, and religious modes of existence establishes that authentic selfhood requires decision that cannot be mediated by reflection alone.

[12] Jean-Paul Sartre, *Being and Nothingness* (1943), Part One, Chapter 2, "Bad Faith." Sartre's analysis shows how consciousness can flee from its freedom into self-deception, treating itself as a thing (facticity) rather than as the nihilating activity (transcendence) that it is. The famous example of the waiter who plays at being a waiter appears in this chapter.

[13] Heidegger's analysis of *Augenblick* (the moment of vision) appears in *Being and Time*, Division II, Chapter 4 (§65). The *Augenblick* is the authentic present in which Dasein seizes its possibilities in resolute decision.

[31] Emmanuel Levinas, *Totality and Infinity: An Essay on Exteriority*, trans. Alphonso Lingis (Pittsburgh: Duquesne University Press, 1969; first published 1961). Levinas's account holds that the face of the Other is not an object of cognition but an ethical summons irreducible to my representations — "prior to every initiative," it "calls into question" the totalizing freedom of the knowing ego. Crucially for the use in §32f-bis, Levinas himself draws the line from this ethical relation to truth: in the Preface he writes that "ethics is an optics" — "a vision without image, bereft of the synoptic and totalizing properties of vision" — and that the face-to-face is the relation out of which objective, non-totalizing discourse and the work of justice become possible. The order is strict, and the book preserves it: for Levinas ethics is prior to and conditions truth, never the reverse. The book therefore uses answerability to the Other as the prior condition of truthful disclosure that a corrupt-but-coherent trajectory forecloses — Levinas's own direction of grounding — rather than as a check operating within a disclosure apparatus, which would invert his order and reduce first philosophy to a subroutine. Like IIT (§55-bis.4), Jonas (note [8]), and Haugeland (note [68]), Levinas is here a witness for the connection the book needs, not a source it strains. One further element of his account must be named to avoid a silent flattening: in Levinas the face's claim is unconditional because the face is where the divine opens — "the dimension of the divine opens forth from the human face" (*Totality and Infinity*, Section I.B, "The Metaphysical and the Human," p. 78): there "the Transcendent, infinitely other, solicits us and appeals to us." So the universality the book needs against every gerrymander has, in its source, an explicitly transcendent ground — though a ground of a striking kind: Levinas insists in the same pages that the relation is social rather than numinous, "as distinct from objectification as from participation," and that monotheist faith itself "implies metaphysical atheism" — the I must remain separate, not absorbed; the relation with the Transcendent passes through the face of the other person, not around it. (The "trace" vocabulary often associated with this point belongs to Levinas's later work; *Totality and Infinity*'s own terms are the idea of infinity and the dimension of the divine.) §32f-bis takes the structure of unconditional answerability while bracketing that ground, and marks the bracket: the deferred question of how universality is secured without it belongs to the "another book" the section names (see also Chapter 17 §79d).


## Chapter 9: The Sedimentation of Meaning

[14] The concept of "sedimentation" (*Sedimentierung*) is developed by Husserl in *Experience and Judgment* (1939) and in *The Crisis of European Sciences*. It names the process by which active meaning-constitution settles into passive habituality, becoming the tacit background for future experience.

[15] John Dewey, *Experience and Nature* (1925) and *Art as Experience* (1934). Dewey's concept of "funded experience" names the accumulated significance that past experience deposits into present perception and action.

[16] Merleau-Ponty's account of the "habitual body" appears in *Phenomenology of Perception*, Part One, Chapter 3. The habitual body is the sedimented layer of acquired skills and dispositions that shapes our engagement with the world.

[17] Hans-Georg Gadamer, *Truth and Method* (1960), Part Two. Gadamer's concept of "effective history" (*Wirkungsgeschichte*) names the way understanding is always already shaped by the history of effects that precedes and conditions it.


## Chapter 10: Truth as Disclosure

[18] Heidegger's interpretation of *aletheia* (unconcealment) as the primordial meaning of truth appears throughout his work, especially in *Being and Time* §44 and in the later essay "On the Essence of Truth" (1930). Against the correspondence theory, Heidegger argues that truth is fundamentally the event of disclosure whereby beings come to show themselves.

[19] Kierkegaard, *Concluding Unscientific Postscript to Philosophical Fragments* (1846), trans. David F. Swenson and Walter Lowrie (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1941). The classic definition: "An objective uncertainty held fast in an appropriation-process of the most passionate inwardness is the truth, the highest truth attainable for an existing individual." This is routinely misread as relativism — truth reduced to preference or feeling — but Kierkegaard's own contrast forecloses that reading: "In the case of a mathematical proposition the objectivity is given, but for this reason the truth of such a proposition is also an indifferent truth." The objective uncertainty remains objective; what "subjective truth" relocates is the *site* of binding truth for an existing individual — the *how* of appropriation rather than the *what* of content. The book inherits this how-not-what structure and its anti-relativist defense (see §42d) while grounding the "how" in irreversible foreclosure rather than Kierkegaard's passionate inwardness. (Quotations follow the Swenson/Lowrie translation; the Hong & Hong edition — Princeton, 1992 — renders these passages in different words, so the wording above should not be cited as Hong & Hong.) The deeper placement in §42d draws on a different work: *The Sickness Unto Death* (1849, published under the pseudonym Anti-Climacus), whose formula for the self free of despair — the self that, "by relating itself to its own self and by willing to be itself," is "grounded transparently in the Power which posited it" — follows the Walter Lowrie translation (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1941). The Hong & Hong edition (Princeton, 1980) renders the passage in different words and has not been collated here; the wording above should not be cited as Hong & Hong. For Kierkegaard the God-relation is the constitution of selfhood, not merely its discipline: a self related only to itself and to other finite selves is, on his account, in despair regardless of its coherence. The book brackets this constitutive claim as a scope decision (§79d) rather than adjudicating it.


## Chapter 11: What AI Is

[20] For a comprehensive account of how large language models function, see the technical literature on transformer architectures. The key point for our purposes is that these systems operate through statistical pattern-matching over learned distributions, without the semantic understanding their outputs might suggest.


## Chapter 12: The Objections

[21] John Searle, "Minds, Brains, and Programs," *Behavioral and Brain Sciences* 3, no. 3 (1980): 417-424. Searle's Chinese Room argument demonstrates that syntactic manipulation of symbols (computation) is insufficient for semantic understanding (meaning).

[22] The functionalist position in philosophy of mind holds that mental states are defined by their functional roles—by their causal relations to inputs, outputs, and other mental states—rather than by their material substrate. Key statements of this view appear in Hilary Putnam, "The Nature of Mental States" (1967), and Jerry Fodor, *The Language of Thought* (1975).

[23] The "4E" approach to cognition—arguing that cognition is Embodied, Embedded, Enactive, and Extended—is developed in Francisco Varela, Evan Thompson, and Eleanor Rosch, *The Embodied Mind* (1991); Andy Clark, *Being There* (1997); and Andy Clark and David Chalmers, "The Extended Mind" (1998).

[24] Hubert Dreyfus, *What Computers Can't Do* (1972) and *What Computers Still Can't Do* (1992). Dreyfus argues, drawing on Heidegger and Merleau-Ponty, that AI cannot replicate human intelligence because it lacks embodied coping—the pre-reflective, skillful engagement with environment that characterizes human being-in-the-world.

[25] Gadamer's concept of the "fusion of horizons" (*Horizontverschmelzung*) appears in *Truth and Method*, Part Two. Understanding occurs when the horizon of the interpreter fuses with the horizon of what is to be understood, producing a new, expanded horizon.

[26] Ludwig Wittgenstein, *Philosophical Investigations* (1953), especially §§138-242 on rule-following. Wittgenstein's analysis shows that following a rule cannot be reduced to matching patterns, because any finite set of applications is compatible with indefinitely many rules.

[27] Alasdair MacIntyre, *After Virtue* (1981). MacIntyre argues that moral reasoning is always tradition-constituted—that there is no neutral standpoint from which to adjudicate between competing traditions.

[54] Derek Parfit, *Reasons and Persons* (Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1984), Part III. Parfit argues that personal identity is not what matters in survival; what matters is Relation R — psychological connectedness and/or continuity, "with any cause" — which can hold one-to-many (the fission/division cases), so that R can obtain where identity does not. R is multiply realizable and cause-indifferent: Parfit's quasi-memory apparatus (e.g. the case of memory-traces copied from one brain to another) is designed precisely to show that R-content and its connections can be transferred across substrates without presupposing a persisting entity or the original causal history of the remembered events. The thought experiments of teletransportation, fission, and gradual replacement are offered as evidence that our ordinary attachment to particular continuation is an illusion. The argument is directed at a "Non-Reductionist" view of persons (broadly Cartesian), and its foundational friendliness to copyability is what makes it the sharpest challenge to §53d-bis; see §53e. The book's reply (§53e.5) grants Parfit's account of survival in full — R is what matters in survival, and R is copyable — and locates the AI exclusion not in survival but in *bearing*, the having-undergone of foreclosure that quasi-memory copies R while leaving behind; the walked trajectory is therefore neither a further entity (not Cartesian) nor reducible to R-content (not copyable), because bearing is not content at all. For a representative collection of the counterweight position, see Sydney Shoemaker and Richard Swinburne, *Personal Identity* (Oxford: Blackwell, 1984). Galen Strawson's "Against Narrativity" (*Ratio* 17, no. 4 [2004]: 428–452) presses a related challenge to narrative-identity views from a different angle; our "walked trajectory" formulation in §53e.6 is not a narrative-identity claim but a claim about what must have occurred for psychological continuity (in either Parfit's or Williams's sense) to exist at all.

[55] Bernard Williams, "The Self and the Future," *Philosophical Review* 79, no. 2 (1970): 161–180; collected in Williams's *Problems of the Self* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1973). Williams argued that when Parfit-style cases are considered from the first-person perspective—imagining *being* the subject rather than judging the case from outside—the dread of non-continuation does not dissolve as the rational reconstruction predicts. The phenomenology resists the reduction. See also Williams's *Moral Luck* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1981) for related work on the limits of impartial ethical reasoning. Our use of Williams is corroborative rather than foundational: the primary response to Parfit (§53e.2) is methodological, not phenomenological; Williams's observation serves as independent evidence that the phenomenology is tracking something the third-person reconstruction misses.

[51] The animal cognition literature relevant to the framework's structures is substantial. On corvid cognition, social intelligence, and apparent responses to death, see Nicola S. Clayton and Nathan J. Emery, "The Mentality of Crows: Convergent Evolution of Intelligence in Corvids and Apes," *Science* 306 (2004): 1903–1907; and Nicola S. Clayton and Anthony Dickinson, "Episodic-like Memory During Cache Recovery by Scrub Jays," *Nature* 395 (1998): 272–274. On elephant responses to conspecific death, Karen McComb, Lucy Baker, and Cynthia Moss, "African Elephants Show High Levels of Interest in the Skulls and Ivory of Their Own Species," *Biology Letters* 2 (2006): 26–28; and more broadly G. A. Bradshaw, *Elephants on the Edge: What Animals Teach Us About Humanity* (New Haven: Yale University Press, 2009). On chimpanzee thanatology, James R. Anderson, Alasdair Gillies, and Louise C. Lock, "Pan Thanatology," *Current Biology* 20 (2010): R349–R351, and Dora Biro et al., "Chimpanzee Mothers at Bossou, Guinea, Carry the Mummified Remains of Their Dead Infants," *Current Biology* 20 (2010): R351–R352. On primate sociality and moral emotion, Frans de Waal, *Primates and Philosophers: How Morality Evolved* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 2006). On cetacean culture and individual identity across generations, Hal Whitehead and Luke Rendell, *The Cultural Lives of Whales and Dolphins* (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 2015). On octopus cognition and the philosophical implications of radically different neural architectures, Peter Godfrey-Smith, *Other Minds: The Octopus, the Sea, and the Deep Origins of Consciousness* (New York: Farrar, Straus and Giroux, 2016). The treatment in §56b-bis does not settle any of the contested questions these literatures raise; it uses them to establish that the framework's criterion is empirically investigable across the animal kingdom rather than stipulated to pick out humans.

[52] The ethical implications of extending disclosure-access to some non-human animals are substantial and fall outside this book's scope. If the framework is right and some animals genuinely satisfy the structural criteria for Meaningful Truth, our obligations to them are substantively different from what consumer-welfare frameworks admit. A full treatment is another book—consistent with how we have handled the artificial-life question (Chapter 17 §79a). What matters for the present argument is that the criterion is principled and admits of application by the evidence rather than by species membership.

[53] David Chalmers, *The Conscious Mind: In Search of a Fundamental Theory* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1996); and earlier "Facing Up to the Problem of Consciousness," *Journal of Consciousness Studies* 2, no. 3 (1995): 200–219. Chalmers's "hard problem" distinguishes the *easy* problems of consciousness (explaining cognitive functions: discrimination, integration, reportability) from the hard problem of phenomenal experience itself—why there is something it is like to undergo any of these processes. Our use of Chalmers in §55-bis is corroborative rather than foundational and does not commit the book to property dualism: we draw on the structural form of his argument (something resists the third-person reduction) without endorsing his metaphysical resolution.

[56] Giulio Tononi, "Consciousness as Integrated Information: A Provisional Manifesto," *Biological Bulletin* 215 (2008): 216–242; Giulio Tononi and Christof Koch, "Consciousness: Here, There and Everywhere?" *Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society B* 370 (2014): 20140167; Masafumi Oizumi, Larissa Albantakis, and Giulio Tononi, "From the Phenomenology to the Mechanisms of Consciousness: Integrated Information Theory 3.0," *PLOS Computational Biology* 10, no. 5 (2014): e1003588. IIT identifies consciousness with integrated information (Φ), understood as the intrinsic, irreducible cause-effect structure of a physical substrate. Contrary to a common misreading, IIT is *not* substrate-neutral in the functionalist sense: because Φ tracks intrinsic cause-effect power rather than computed function, the theory holds that a digital computer perfectly simulating a conscious brain "would experience next to nothing" — "in sharp contrast to widespread functionalist beliefs" — and that a behaviorally equivalent feed-forward system is a "perfect zombie" (Tononi and Koch 2014/2015; cf. Christof Koch, "What Is Consciousness?" *Nature* 557 [2018]: S8–S12). Our engagement in §55-bis therefore treats IIT not as a rival but as an independent witness, from inside the science of consciousness, for the negative claim that function does not suffice for the inner phenomenon — while noting that IIT and the book differ on what is missing (intrinsic cause-effect power vs. existential structure). The argument does not depend on IIT's truth either way: even granting IIT in full, consciousness so identified is not situatedness, and the structures disclosure-truth requires remain absent.

[57] Bernard Baars, *A Cognitive Theory of Consciousness* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1988), and Stanislas Dehaene, *Consciousness and the Brain: Deciphering How the Brain Codes Our Thoughts* (New York: Viking, 2014), develop and extend Global Workspace Theory. On predictive processing, see Karl Friston, "The Free-Energy Principle: A Unified Brain Theory?" *Nature Reviews Neuroscience* 11 (2010): 127–138; Andy Clark, *Surfing Uncertainty: Prediction, Action, and the Embodied Mind* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2016); Jakob Hohwy, *The Predictive Mind* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2013); and Anil Seth, *Being You: A New Science of Consciousness* (London: Faber & Faber, 2021). Predictive processing is closer in spirit to the embodied-coping tradition this book draws on than IIT or GWT; it is not a threat to the argument and may, in some formulations, be supportive.

[58] Patrick Butlin et al., "Consciousness in Artificial Intelligence: Insights from the Science of Consciousness," arXiv preprint arXiv:2308.08708 (2023). The survey applies indicators drawn from major contemporary theories of consciousness (Global Workspace Theory, Higher-Order Theories, Predictive Processing, Attention Schema Theory) to current AI systems and concludes that no current AI system clearly satisfies any theory's indicators for consciousness. Our engagement in §55-bis does not depend on this conclusion but is consistent with it; the book's structural argument is independent of whichever empirical conclusion the consciousness-in-AI investigation eventually reaches.

[63] On the contemporary computationalism named in §52a beyond Putnam and Fodor: David Lewis, *Philosophical Papers, Volume I* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1983), particularly "Psychophysical and Theoretical Identifications" and "Mad Pain and Martian Pain"; Zenon Pylyshyn, *Computation and Cognition: Toward a Foundation for Cognitive Science* (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 1984); Patricia Churchland, *Neurophilosophy: Toward a Unified Science of the Mind-Brain* (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 1986); Paul Churchland, *Matter and Consciousness*, rev. ed. (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 1988). The framework's response to functionalism is structural across these variants and does not turn on details specific to any one of them.

[64] Alva Noë, *Action in Perception* (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 2004). Noë's sensorimotor enactivism holds that perceiving just *is* the exercise of practical sensorimotor know-how — perception cannot be peeled off from skilled bodily engagement. The position is closer to the framework here than any computational theory, and the response in §54c-bis grants the structural insight while pressing the further question of vulnerable, non-optional continuity.

[65] Daniel D. Hutto and Erik Myin, *Radicalizing Enactivism: Basic Minds Without Content* (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 2013). Radical enactivism presses further than sensorimotor enactivism: basic minds do not contend with content at all; cognition is world-involving activity, not representation-manipulation. The framework's engagement with radical enactivism in §54c-bis is sympathetic on the anti-representationalist point and divergent on whether such activity by itself yields disclosure-truth.

[66] Evan Thompson, *Mind in Life: Biology, Phenomenology, and the Sciences of Mind* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 2007). Thompson's autopoietic enactivism develops Varela's earlier work into a full philosophy of biology: mind is the form of life; sense-making is what self-maintaining systems do in virtue of being self-maintaining. Of all contemporary positions in philosophy of mind, this is the one closest to the framework's own commitments. The disagreement is narrow: whether sensorimotor coupling alone (without the further structures the book identifies) is sufficient for disclosure-truth.

[67] On the lineage from Hans Jonas's metabolism argument (see [8]) to Thompson's autopoietic enactivism: Thompson explicitly draws on Jonas in *Mind in Life*, particularly in the chapters on autopoiesis and sense-making. The framework here shares this lineage and acknowledges the convergence; the divergence in §54c-bis is over the sufficiency claim, not the structural insight.

[68] John Haugeland, *Having Thought: Essays in the Metaphysics of Mind* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1998), particularly "Truth and Rule-Following" (§§5, 11, 13, 16–18), where the account of existential commitment and beholdenness is developed. Haugeland develops the existential reading of computation: the human-AI difference is not primarily about embodied skill but about *commitment* — the way a thinker is bound by what its thoughts are answerable to, "on pain of giving up the game." The relation to the present book requires care, since this is the strongest case for the charge that the book's central move is inherited. On examination it is not: Haugeland's existential commitment is a *standing condition* — "a dedicated or even devoted way of living," "a determination to maintain and carry on," whose temporal structure throughout §§16–18 is durative (maintenance, persistence, ongoing beholdenness to a check that can "bring the system down"). The book's decision-as-severance is the *event* — the irreversible foreclosure that constitutes the decider in the cutting — which Haugeland's continuation-vocabulary does not isolate. His "on pain of giving up the game" gestures toward foreclosure, and a specialist might argue that to commit is implicitly to have cut; the book grants this and makes the narrower claim that it isolates and thematizes the constitutive severance as the *mechanism* of disclosure, which Haugeland leaves implicit. Note too that Haugeland's own term "disclosure" (§11) names the opening-up of a domain as playable — closer to what the book calls field-expansion (§75f) than to the cut. On lineage, Haugeland explicitly aligns himself with Heidegger and against the pragmatists ("antipragmatists such as Heidegger and I," §5) and works in a Sellars-and-Kant-inflected register — the same lineage the book claims.

[69] Michael Wheeler, *Reconstructing the Cognitive World: The Next Step* (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 2005). Wheeler extends the Heideggerian framework into contemporary cognitive science, distinguishing genuinely embodied-embedded systems from those that merely simulate embodiment. He should be placed precisely rather than enlisted as a flat ally, because his project is *reconstructive*, not eliminative: a Heideggerian *cognitive science* built on four positive commitments (online intelligence as primary, generated through brain-body-environment causal spread, with heightened biological sensitivity and a dynamical-systems perspective), with representation reconstructed as action-oriented rather than rejected, and with Haugeland's refrigerator (reaching past the milk for the juice) as the paradigm of online intelligence. Wheeler is therefore more optimistic than the book about whether the Dreyfusian critique can be met by an appropriately embodied-embedded cognitive science and the artificial systems continuous with it. The book takes his genuine-versus-simulated distinction while parting from him on the prospects (§54d): where Wheeler reconstructs, the book holds that scalable, copyable, functionally individuated computation, however embodied-embedded, still lacks the borne trajectory disclosure-truth requires.


## Chapter 13: What AI Actually Is

[28] Franz Brentano, *Psychology from an Empirical Standpoint* (1874). Brentano's thesis of intentionality—that mental states are distinguished by their "directedness" toward objects—became foundational for phenomenology.

[29] The characterization of AI as "mirrors that move" is original to this book, though it resonates with various critiques of AI as sophisticated mimicry. The point is that AI reflects patterns from training data without the vision that would make reflection meaningful.


## Chapter 14: Superintelligence Reconsidered

[30] The standard picture of superintelligence assumes recursive self-improvement leading to capabilities beyond human comprehension. For representative statements, see Nick Bostrom, *Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies* (2014). Our argument suggests this picture conflates processing power with access to Meaningful Truth.

[70] Nick Bostrom, *Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014). Bostrom's central concerns — the *orthogonality thesis* (intelligence and final goals are independent) and *instrumental convergence* (instrumental subgoals such as self-preservation arise across many final goals) — frame the contemporary AI-risk discourse. The engagement in §65d concedes the structural shape of the danger Bostrom identifies (formal objective-directedness produces hard-to-oversee optimization) while distinguishing this from existential goalhood in the framework's sense.

[71] Stuart Russell, *Human Compatible: Artificial Intelligence and the Problem of Control* (New York: Viking, 2019). Russell proposes a research program built on three principles: the machine's only objective is to maximize the realization of human preferences; the machine is initially uncertain about what those preferences are; and the ultimate source of information about human preferences is human behavior. The mechanism is inverse reinforcement learning — inferring preferences from observed behavior rather than being given an explicit objective — formalized through cooperative "assistance games" (see also Stuart Russell, Daniel Hadfield-Menell, Anca Dragan, and Pieter Abbeel, "The Off-Switch Game," IJCAI 2017, and "Cooperative Inverse Reinforcement Learning," NeurIPS 2016). On Russell's account, corrigibility — a machine's willingness to be switched off or corrected — is not engineered in directly but falls out of the machine's uncertainty about human preferences: a machine that knows it might be wrong about what humans want has reason to defer. (The book does not attribute to Russell the distinct "inverse reward design" proposal of Hadfield-Menell et al., *Inverse Reward Design*, NeurIPS 2017, which addresses misspecified reward functions rather than Russell's preference-uncertainty framework.) The framework endorses Russell's operational guidance while redescribing the underlying picture: humans must remain answerable for deployment because we are the locus of normative weight, not because the AI is a moral agent whose values must be shaped.

[72] Eliezer Yudkowsky's writings on AI risk are dispersed across the Machine Intelligence Research Institute's publications and various long-form essays; for a representative collection, see Eliezer Yudkowsky, *Rationality: From AI to Zombies* (Berkeley: Machine Intelligence Research Institute, 2015). Yudkowsky's emphasis on existential risk from misaligned superintelligence has shaped much of the field's framing. The framework's engagement in §65d is with the broad line of argument, not with any particular formulation.

[73] Paul Christiano, "Clarifying 'AI Alignment,'" *AI Alignment Forum* (2018), and "Supervising Strong Learners by Amplifying Weak Experts," with Buck Shlegeris and Dario Amodei, arXiv:1810.08575 (2018); see also the work on debate (Geoffrey Irving, Paul Christiano, and Dario Amodei, "AI Safety via Debate," arXiv:1805.00899 [2018]). Christiano's research program on alignment, debate, and corrigibility is among the most technically developed in contemporary AI safety. The framework treats this work as important and redescribes its rationale: powerful optimization must remain answerable to beings capable of bearing normative weight, not because AI has owned goals that require moral education.


## Chapter 15: What This Means for Us

[33] Sartre, *Existentialism is a Humanism* (1946). Sartre's famous claim that humans are "condemned to be free" captures the burden and dignity of existence: we cannot escape the responsibility of choosing what we will become.


## Chapter 16: The Centaur

[32] The centaur metaphor for human-AI collaboration originates with Garry Kasparov's "Advanced Chess" (proposed in the late 1990s after his 1997 loss to Deep Blue), in which human-computer teams competed against unassisted humans and unassisted computers. The centaur teams won. Kasparov's metaphor framed the asymmetry performatively—human intuition plus computer search beats either alone. This chapter argues that the asymmetry is actually ontological: the human half of the centaur is the only bearer of weight in the system, and this is what makes the collaboration meaningful rather than merely more capable.

[76] The postphenomenological theory of technological mediation originates with Don Ihde, *Technology and the Lifeworld: From Garden to Earth* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1990), and is developed into an ethics by Peter-Paul Verbeek, *What Things Do* (University Park: Pennsylvania State University Press, 2005), and especially *Moralizing Technology: Understanding and Designing the Morality of Things* (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 2011). The obstetric-ultrasound case is introduced in *Moralizing Technology*, chapter 2, and revisited in chapter 4; the underlying theory of mediation and distributed agency is in chapter 3. The quoted phrases — that ultrasound "constitutes expectant parents as decision makers regarding the life of their unborn child," and that "what appears to be an innocent look into the womb may end up being the first step in a decisionmaking process that the expectant couple did not explicitly choose" — are from chapter 2 (pp. 26–27). Verbeek there already presses the framing-as-foreclosure to its edge, noting that "the choice not to have an ultrasound scan made is also a choice" and that the situation is "irreversibly" changed; the book credits him with this structure and claims no priority over it. Verbeek's distribution of agency across the human-technology hybrid draws on Bruno Latour, *We Have Never Been Modern*, trans. Catherine Porter (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1993), and "Where Are the Missing Masses? The Sociology of a Few Mundane Artifacts," in *Shaping Technology / Building Society*, ed. Wiebe Bijker and John Law (Cambridge, MA: MIT Press, 1992), 225–258. The book's departure is twofold (§75f): the framing is distributed but the *bearing* is not — against Verbeek's hybrid distribution of agency and his design-ethical openness, via Achterhuis, to delegating decisions to devices; and the decider-side corrective is the bearing of a distributed foreclosure rather than the Foucauldian styling of one's mediated subjectivity (Verbeek, *Moralizing Technology*, chapter 4).


## Explanatory Notes: Technical Terms

[34] **Dasein**: Literally "being-there" or "being-the-there." Heidegger uses this term rather than "human" or "subject" to avoid importing Cartesian assumptions. Dasein names the kind of being whose being is an issue for it—the being that exists by asking what it means to be.

[35] **Aletheia**: Usually translated "truth"; Heidegger reads it through its privative form (*a-* [not] + *lēthē* [forgetting/concealment]) as "un-concealment" or "un-hiddenness"—the emergence of what was hidden into manifestness rather than the correspondence of statement to fact. Two cautions. The etymology is contested among classicists, and the equation of *aletheia* with "truth" shifted across Heidegger's own career: *Being and Time* §44 treats disclosedness as the *most primordial* phenomenon of truth, grounding (not rivaling) correspondence; the late Heidegger, in "The End of Philosophy and the Task of Thinking" (1964; in *On Time and Being*, trans. Joan Stambaugh, New York: Harper & Row, 1972), withdrew the word "truth" from unconcealment altogether, calling it the opening that "first grants the possibility of truth"—the very point at which, since Plato, the essence of truth had narrowed from the unconcealment of beings to the correctness of the proposition. There he disavows his own earlier identification in plain words: "it was inadequate and misleading to call *aletheia*, in the sense of opening, truth." This book follows the late correction: disclosure is the *ground* of truth, not a fourth kind of it (see §41e).

[36] **Sorge** (care): Not emotional caring-about but ontological structure: Dasein is the being whose being is at issue for it, who cannot be indifferent to its own existence. Care names this self-referential structure that makes stakes possible.

[37] **Geworfenheit** (thrownness): From German *werfen*, to throw. We find ourselves already "thrown" into existence—into a body, a historical moment, a language, a culture—without having chosen any of it. This thrown condition is not obstacle but ground.

[38] **Zuhandenheit** (ready-to-hand): The mode of being of equipment in use. The hammer being hammered with is not an object contemplated but a transparent extension of activity. Contrasts with *Vorhandenheit* (present-at-hand)—the mode of being of objects explicitly noticed, typically in breakdown.

[39] **Nichtigkeit** (nullity): The "not-ness" that structures existence. To be one thing is to not-be another; to choose this path is to nullify that one. Nullity is not deficiency but constitutive structure of finite being.

[40] **Néantisation**: Sartre's term for the "nihilating" activity of consciousness. Consciousness introduces a gap (nothingness) between itself and what it is conscious of, enabling reflection but also bad faith—the denial of what one is by claiming distance from it.

[41] **Corps vécu** (lived body): The body as lived, as distinct from the objective body (*Körper*) studied by physiology. Merleau-Ponty insists we do not *have* bodies; we *are* bodily—the body is subject, not object.


## Explanatory Notes: Clarifications

[42] On Hume's is-ought passage: Hume's actual passage is notoriously compressed. He observes that moral philosophers shift imperceptibly from "is" statements to "ought" statements without explaining the transition. Whether Hume intended this as a logical point (you *cannot* derive ought from is) or merely a methodological complaint (philosophers *should* explain the derivation) remains debated. We follow the stronger interpretation, which has dominated subsequent discussion.

[43] On Moore's Open Question Argument: Moore's argument has been challenged on multiple grounds—that it proves too much (making *any* definition of "good" impossible), that it relies on questionable semantic assumptions, that naturalists can bite the bullet and accept that the question *is* closed for competent speakers. We cannot resolve this debate here. Our point is narrower: the persistence of the challenge indicates that normativity resists easy reduction, which is what the gap's existence reveals.

[44] On "the path is the point": This phrase risks sounding like self-help cliché ("it's about the journey, not the destination"). We mean something more precise: truth is not separable from the path that discloses it. The destination (propositional conclusion) without the path (committed traversal) is not the same truth—is information *about* what the path discloses, not the disclosure itself. The path is literally constitutive of the truth, not merely instrumental to reaching it.

[45] On grief as structural marker: We do not claim that every decision is accompanied by conscious grief. Many decisions are small, their foreclosures trivial. The claim is structural: where genuine decision occurs (irreversible foreclosure transforming the decider), something like grief—the somatic registration of loss—marks the cut. In small decisions, this may be barely perceptible. In large decisions, it can be overwhelming. The absence of *any* such registration suggests the "decision" was not genuine—was selection rather than severance.

[46] On phenomenology vs. science: A skeptical reader might object: "This is all just phenomenology—descriptions of how things *seem*, not how they *are*. Why should I trust first-person reports over third-person science?" The objection presupposes that third-person methods access reality while first-person methods access mere appearance. But this is precisely what we contest. Third-person methods access one dimension of reality (what shows up from detached observation); first-person methods access another (what shows up in engaged existence). Neither is "mere" appearance. Both are modes of disclosure.


## Explanatory Notes: Historical Context

[47] On Husserl's *Crisis*: Husserl wrote *The Crisis of European Sciences* in the mid-1930s, as Nazism rose in Germany. The "crisis" he diagnosed was not merely academic: he saw the reduction of human existence to mathematized nature as connected to the broader dehumanization of his era. Husserl, of Jewish descent, was stripped of his academic positions; he died in 1938. The *Crisis* remained unfinished.

[48] On Heidegger's politics: Heidegger's involvement with National Socialism (he joined the Nazi Party in 1933 and served as rector of Freiburg University) casts a permanent shadow over his work. The decisionism charge — that the resoluteness of *Being and Time* is contentless and could attach itself to any politics, with 1933 as the demonstration — was pressed first by Karl Löwith ("The Political Implications of Heidegger's Existentialism," 1946) and later by Jürgen Habermas ("Work and Weltanschauung: The Heidegger Controversy from a German Perspective," *Critical Inquiry* 15, 1989); both are collected in Richard Wolin, ed., *The Heidegger Controversy: A Critical Reader* (New York: Columbia University Press, 1991). The book does not leave the matter at acknowledgment: §32f-bis runs Heidegger's own trajectory through the framework's answerability condition as its canonical test case — the borne-but-totalizing path, convicted not by lack of integrity of traversal but by gerrymandered answerability. Our use of his concepts does not endorse his actions or the totality of his thought; the framework's relation to its founder's failure is argued in the body, not waved off here.

[49] On Kierkegaard's pseudonyms: Kierkegaard published many of his works under pseudonyms (Johannes de Silentio for *Fear and Trembling*, Judge William for portions of *Either/Or*, etc.). These are not mere pen names but distinct "authors" with their own perspectives, which Kierkegaard used to present existential positions without endorsing them in his own voice. Scholarly debate continues about how to attribute views expressed pseudonymously.

[50] On Searle's Chinese Room: Searle's thought experiment has generated enormous literature—systems replies, robot replies, brain simulator replies, and Searle's responses to each. We cite it not to enter this debate but to illustrate a point about simulation: that producing outputs matching a function is not the same as *being* what produces those outputs in the original case. Whether the Chinese Room succeeds as an argument against Strong AI is separate from whether it illuminates the simulation/instantiation distinction.




# Bibliography


## Primary Sources

### Phenomenology and Existentialism

Brentano, Franz. *Psychology from an Empirical Standpoint*. Translated by Antos C. Rancurello, D.B. Terrell, and Linda L. McAlister. London: Routledge, 1995. First published 1874.
